10.1016/j.jmp.2013.03.003
In recent years, numerous researchers have highlighted the conceptual, mathematical, and statistical gap between algebraic axioms underlying theory, such as for utility, and the associated empirical data. Several authors have called this issue “Luce’s challenge” because R. Duncan Luce has been a leader in encouraging the field to tackle these problems.  Luce’s (1995, 1997)  challenge can be partially summarized as: (1) recast a deterministic theory as a probabilistic model; and (2) properly test that probabilistic model of the deterministic theory on available data. In this paper, we consider a problem that might be thought of as a type of inverse of the first component of Luce’s challenge: (3) recast a probabilistic model as the result of a maximization process over a deterministic representation.

There is a vast literature on deterministic theory for utility and preference as evidenced, for example, by the two-volume  Handbook of Utility Theory  ( Barbera, Hammond, & Seidl, 1999, 2004 ); more recently,  Wakker (2010)  is an excellent source on the subject. The corresponding literature on probabilistic theories is also extensive, though only lately has significant progress been made in formulating probabilistic models that might reasonably be considered tests of deterministic theory ( Regenwetter, Dana, & Davis-Stober, 2011; Regenwetter & Davis-Stober, 2008 ). The larger part of the literature on stochastic models of choice has simply combined a convenient random or constant utility model with some representation from the deterministic literature ( Fishburn, 2002; Stott, 2006; Wilcox, 2008 ).

The interpretation of stochasticity in such models has been rather different for Economists and Psychologists. Economists tend to assume that variability in choice indicates that there is information used by decision makers (DMs) that is not available to researchers; Psychologists assume that an individual DM may very well not be consistent across repeated choices between the same options, variously because of limited cognitive ability, lack of attention, and/or changing tastes. Nonetheless, even with this difference in perspective, both disciplines have tended to focus on similar classes of model, the two most common being the  probit model  ( Bockenholt, 2006; Daganzo, 1979 ) and the  logit model  and other members of the  generalized extreme value class  ( Louviere, Hensher, & Swait, 2000; Luce, 1959, 1977; McFadden, 1976 ). Marketing, transportation and applied fields of economics,  inter alia , have made extensive use of such models, especially in the area of  discrete choice experiments  ( Louviere et al., 2000 ); one component of that work is to use such  stated preferences  (discrete choice experiments) to predict  revealed preferences  (choices in the market place).

Most of the theoretical and empirical work on probabilistic choice is based on  random utility models . The most basic of such models assumes each of a currently available set of choice options is characterized by a valuation that has a deterministic (systematic) component plus a random component determined by some specified distribution. Psychologists usually assume that the random component is consistent (in a well-defined manner) across all the choice subsets under study, but this is often less clearly articulated by Economists. Then, on each choice opportunity, the option chosen is the one with the maximum total value (i.e., the sum of the systematic and the random components). A particular random utility model is then fully specified by the selection of the distribution of the random component.

In general, the chosen distribution is  not  motivated by significant conceptual reasons, but rather for matters of convenience. The major examples are  Generalized Extreme Value  distributions, which yield closed forms for the choice probabilities, and  Multivariate Normal  distributions which can easily incorporate general covariance patterns. The major exception to the statement that the distribution of the random component is chosen for convenience is the use of the Type I Extreme Value (also called Gumbel or double exponential; for

This is because, for choice probabilities that are all nonzero,  Luce’s choice axiom  (1959) is equivalent to the MNL model, which in turn implies under relatively weak mathematical assumptions that the random component is distributed as Extreme Value ( Yellott, 1977 ).

As summarized above, random utility models of choice are based on the idea of utility maximization, where the utilities have a random component (not necessarily additive, but usually so specified). Two other general approaches have been proposed for deriving probabilistic choice from deterministic representations. The first assumes that the deterministic representation of the available options are such that a lottery over the available options is (can be) preferred to any of those options.  Machina (1985)  arrives at such probabilistic choice by arguing for indifference curves with a property (quasiconcavity) such that a lottery over the available options is (can be) preferred to any of those options.  Marley (1997)  also achieves probabilistic choice, but by assuming that the DM has multiple goals such that there are (can be) nondominated (Pareto optimal) choices that are lotteries over the offered options; special cases of his results yield various standard utility representations.

The second, and much more common approach employs the maximization of one quantity, subject to constraints on one or more other quantities. We now present an extended non-mathematical summary and discussion of such work as a lead into our related, but new, conceptualization; to our knowledge, such a review has not been presented elsewhere.

Several researchers have considered expected utility maximization under the constraint of some cost function being less than some goal value; or maximization of some measure of variety as given by, say, the Shannon information (entropy) of the responses, under the constraint that the expected utility exceeds some goal value. One of the earliest examples of such an approach is presented in  Erlander (1977) , who assumes that the entropy of a travel (trip) matrix can be used as a broad measure of accessibility within a transportation system, and then assumes that the goal is to minimize average trip cost subject to constraints on the accessibility (entropy)—for instance, that the entropy is greater than some fixed amount. He shows that the solution of this problem yields an exponential function for the number of journeys between zones in the network, with the exponential involving parameters in the Lagrangian formulation of the problem.  Erlander (1982, 1998, 2005)  extend this approach, with  Erlander (2010)  presenting an integrative summary. Building on his earlier work,  Erlander (1998)  introduces the concept of a discrete probability distribution over a set of options, each with some utility value, being  efficient  if and only if a sample from a distribution with higher expected utility is at least as probable as a sample with lower expected utility; he then shows that any efficient discrete distribution has the multinomial logit (MNL) form in the utilities.

There are numerous related derivations of MNL-type choice models in Psychology and Economics (for that matter, in many other fields). These derivations are sometimes based on the  maximum information entropy principle  (often abbreviated to MIP; also known as the  maximum entropy formalism ). This principle derives the distribution that has maximum entropy given certain constraints imposed. For instance,  Miyano (2001)  and  Myung (1994)  use this approach to derive various classic psychological models of categorization by assuming that the observer has various evidences (estimates) about the stimuli in the experiment and the perceptions that arise (probabilistically) from such stimuli.  Cheremukhin, Popova, and Tutino (2013)  and  Matëjka and McKay (2011)  assume that in reaching a decision about which (risky or uncertain) option to select, a DM accumulates costly information about the available options. Both articles assume that the cost is a (not necessarily linear) function of the (eventual) entropy or relative entropy of the choices made.  Matëjka and McKay (2011)  assume that the DM maximizes the expected utility of the options subject to an upper bound on the cost, whereas  Cheremukhin et al. (2013)  assume that the DM maximizes the expected utility of the options minus the cost; as we describe later, the latter approach is related to our own, though each approach leads to different classes of models. Both sets of authors then use the Lagrangian method to show that the maximum is achieved with choice probabilities given by an MNL model (as a special case for Cheremukhin et al.) whose parameters are the utilities of the options and a scalar dependent on the cost function.

Each of the above approaches can be formulated in two (other) equivalent manners: (a) maximize expected utility under a constraint on the entropy or (b) maximize the entropy under a constraint on the expected utility. Each formulation would be restated in terms of a Lagrangian function and the solution would be found by setting its first-order conditions (partial derivatives) to zero. However, in general, such a solution involves parameters that are determined by the constraints, and specific values of those parameters need to be selected (found) for which the constraints hold. This second step is often  not  taken in such an approach, and either the relevant parameters are treated as estimable in the model or certain of them are not identifiable. An exception to this statement is  Miyano (2001, Corollary 1) , who gives explicit solutions for such parameters for special cases of his model. Also, this issue does not arise in certain special cases of the  Cheremukhin et al. (2013)  approach, or our own, as the parameters corresponding to those given by the above Lagrangian approach are specified by the models, and can be estimated from data.

Our formulation avoids these issues by assuming that the DM is attempting to simultaneously achieve multiple goals (a vector optimization problem). Since simultaneous optimization of such (often incompatible) goals is generally not possible, we assume that the DM weights each goal with an  importance weight  and solves the resulting weighted (scalar) version of the vector optimization problem. Under reasonable mathematical conditions, such scalar optimization is known to give a nondominated solution to the original vector optimization problem; in fact, under such conditions, any noninferior solution of a vector optimization problem can be obtained in this manner (see  Chankong and Haimes (1983) , for the underlying theory, also summarized later in this paper; and  Marley (1997) , for applications to stochastic preferential choice). The goal weights, in general, play the same role as the corresponding parameters in the constrained optimization approaches described above, but in our framework they can legitimately be estimated from data as they measure properties of the DM, not of a Lagrangian formulation of a problem of optimization under constraints.

No process models (e.g., psychological, cognitive, or neural) have been formulated for previous developments based on constrained maximization or for ours based on vector optimization. Thus, the models are descriptive (“as if”) accounts of data. However, the probabilistic choice representations obtained by these descriptive approaches can be formulated in terms of process (next paragraph), and we believe that our formulation provides insight and interpretation of those probabilistic choices and the parameters that determine them. In the final standard interpretation of the MNL-form for choice probabilities, the probabilities are conceptualized as a consequence of applying a simple method to approximately choose the option with the maximum utility (or value); in this case the representation is called the  softmax  decision rule ( Botvinick, Niv, & Barto, 2009 ). If one includes a scale factor (often called the “temperature” because of the relation of the MNL-form to a parallel form in thermodynamics), then by changing its value the representation can range between random choice and fully consistent (deterministic) choice of the option with the maximum utility. In the context of preferential choice, this scale factor can be interpreted as the quantity that balances the amount of  exploitation  (large choice probabilities for currently preferred options) versus  exploration  (more equal choice probabilities for all the available options). This perspective on MNL-type models has come to the fore in recent literature on reinforcement learning. For example,  Achbany, Fouss, Yen, Pirotte, and Saerens (2006, 2008)  and  Saerens, Achbany, Fouss, and Yen (2009)  formulate the exploration/exploitation trade-off as a global optimization problem: find the exploration strategy that minimizes the expected cumulative cost of some set of choices, while maintaining some fixed degree of exploration. They assume an entropy form for the degree of exploration and, unsurprisingly, given the literature summarized above, derive an MNL-type model for the optimal choices. The general topic of exploitation versus exploration in humans is also drawing attention in the neurosciences ( Cohen, McClure, & Yu, 2007; Daw, O’Doherty, Dayan, Seymour, & Dolan, 2006 ) and, of course, continues to be important in the study of animal decision making (see  Stephens (2008) , and the references cited there). There are also a few articles on consumer choice that use entropy as a measure of variety seeking ( Thiel & Weiss, 2003; Weiss, 2010 ), which has a similar interpretation as exploration. Finally,  Hirsh, Mar, and Peterson (2012)  present a generally non-mathematical construct, related to classical entropy, which they call  psychological entropy , defined as the experience of conflicting perceptual and behavioral affordances.

As the above summary of the literature makes clear, the Shannon information measure (entropy) carries much of the weight in the derivations of MNL-type choice probabilities. In general, there is no serious theoretical underpinning for this assumption. The major exception is the work of Aczél, Ng, Luce, and Marley (in various author combinations) on the  utility of gambling  ( Luce, Marley, and Ng (2009) , summarizes much of that work). The basic idea of that axiomatic work is that when a DM is presented with a lottery (gamble), there is not only utility (value) associated with its outcomes (such as winning or losing a certain amount of money), but there is also utility associated with the act of gambling,  per se . Various behavioral conditions (axioms) are then introduced as plausible conditions that a DM might satisfy in such situations, none of which explicitly involve an entropy function. However, the resulting real-valued representations all involve a utility expression (in the generic sense—that is, including forms such as those of prospect theory) plus an unrestricted scalar multiplier of the entropy of the events in the lottery. In fact, when the gambles are risky (i.e., each possible outcome of the gamble has a specified probability), we can reinterpret the real-valued representation as one for a DM who is selecting a set of choice probabilities over a set of available options (consequences); the utility term is then the expected value of those choices, and the entropy term can be interpreted as a measure of variety seeking (or freedom of choice). This is exactly the representation and interpretation proposed in our prototypical decision problem. Maximization of the representation with respect to the choice probabilities then leads to the MNL model (as in earlier related work). More importantly, we show that various more complex members of the generalized extreme value (GEV) class of models (see, e.g.,  Ben-Akiva and Lerman (1985)  and  McFadden (1978) ) can be viewed from our decompositional perspective; these more complex models have not been considered in earlier constrained maximization approaches.

Even though our assumption that variety seeking is measured by entropy can be supported indirectly by the axiomatic work on the utility of gambling, that measure may seem inadequate for describing the choices of a DM seeking variety over various features of, say, yogurt: e.g., brand, taste, and packaging. As we discuss at the end of the paper,  Ng, Luce, and Marley (2007)  discuss a more general representation that may be appropriate for such situations. Interestingly, entropy continues to be a main component of the term that can be interpreted as variety seeking, and the one that drives the choice probabilities in our interpretation, again leading to MNL-type models. Thus, given this commonality for the role of an entropy term, in the body of the paper we present our results for the more basic representation.

To summarize our contribution, the present paper can be seen as integrative of various of the previous results in a framework that conceptualizes the underlying process as the simultaneous pursuit of multiple goals—that is, as vector optimization. Since in many situations there is no strategy that simultaneously achieves all the goals, we assume that the vector optimization problem is transformed to a scalar optimization problem, as is standard (e.g.,  Chankong and Haimes (1983) ). Under reasonable mathematical conditions, the latter yields noninferior (Pareto optimal) solutions to the vector optimization problem: in fact, under such conditions, any noninferior solution of a vector optimization problem can be obtained in this manner (see later in the paper). The weights assumed in the scalar optimization problem are selected by the DM, not by the researcher; in contrast, the conceptually related weights in the Lagrangian versions of the constrained optimization formulations (see above) are selected by the researcher. Thus, in general, in our framework the weights are interpretable as properties of the DM, are often estimable, and could differ in different decision contexts ( Cheremukhin et al. (2013) , fit MNL models derived from their approach to binary choice between gambles; in that special case, their parameter  
                         θ 
                      , determined by the derivative of their cost function, is interpretable in terms of our weights). This approach allows us to give new interpretations to various topics in the literature on discrete choice models, such as why the  inclusive value (or logsum)  is a key construct in the MNL and other generalized extreme value models (see  Ben-Akiva and Lerman (1985)  and  McFadden (1978) ), and to develop results applicable to models in the GEV class that are more general than the MNL—for instance, the nested logit. The proposed approach of simultaneous goal pursuit generates probabilistic choice without the need for arbitrary stochastic assumptions, such as those needed for the derivation of these same models from the random utility perspective. In the paper we also examine extensions to the goal pursuit model that add random variables to the core goal pursuit framework, but reiterate that the core model results are  not  dependent on distributional assumptions concerning stochastic components.

Section   2  develops and demonstrates a multiple goal driven representation of individual choice within which high-level goals (specifically, motivations) are given an explicit role in generating probabilistic choice outcomes from deterministic valuations. Specific formulations of goal-driven decision making are shown to underlie the well-known MNL model. Section   3  presents extensions of the framework to include both the nested logit and a MNL variant that explicitly accounts for prior choices or social influences. Section   4  begins with an in-depth discussion of certain aspects of the framework and presents a number of open issues and directions for future research.

In this section we define a prototype multi-objective (vector) decision problem as an optimization, the solution to which yields the corresponding choice probabilities. We intend this prototype to be the core expression of a decision instance that is both broad enough to be widely applicable because it contains sufficient germane characterization, yet specific enough that it is not overwhelming in details.

For most of the paper, we assume that the DM simultaneously pursues two goals in the process of making choices, each of which can be expressed as a separate optimization problem. The approach can be extended to additional goals, with explicit solutions available for the choice probabilities, provided that one goal remains maximizing the entropy form (below) and each other goal is linear in the choice probabilities.

Now we present each of two goals, one corresponding to consumption (exploitation), the second to variety seeking (exploration):

We now state the form and solution of the scalar optimization framework, then further discuss its reasonableness for our situation.

                               J

                         To deal with the straightforward case  (3) a of only one alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , it is simple to show that the objective function of mathematical program  (1) , the EV goal, achieves its maximum value by assigning all probability mass to the single alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , and none to the remaining alternatives in  
                               C 
                            . Essentially, any probability mass assigned to an alternative not in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             reduces the objective function; hence, all probability mass must be allocated to  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             in an optimal solution. Since there is only one alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , assign all probability mass to it. This solution satisfies the constraints of  (1) , so the optimal probability solution is given by   (5d) . 
                         The more general case  (3) b, in which there are two or more alternatives in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , leads to a much more complex solution for the optimal choice probabilities. Assigning any probability mass to alternatives not in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             will reduce the objective function compared to shifting that mass to an alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , hence it is suboptimal to assign  any  probability mass outside  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            . On the other hand,  any  probability distribution among the alternatives in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             yields the same maximum objective function value. Hence, expression  (5e)  describes a class of feasible probability distributions that maximize   (1) . □

                                     Λ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ̃ 
                                  
                               
                             by adding two weighted linear constraint functions, the Lagrangian is also concave. This implies that  (5b)  is the unique solution to both the Lagrangian and the original optimization problem  (3) .

Case  3:  
                               
                                  
                                     w 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               1

Under this condition, maximizing objective function  (4b)  becomes identical to maximizing only goal EV (expression  (1) ). Separate the alternatives having the maximum value  
                               
                                  
                                     κ 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        V 
                                     
                                     
                                        i 
                                     
                                  
                                  − 
                                  
                                     
                                        τ 
                                     
                                     
                                        i 
                                        C 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                             among  
                               i 
                               ∈ 
                               C 
                             into set  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , as defined in  (5c) ; this set will include at least one and at most  
                               J 
                             alternatives.

To deal with the straightforward case  (3) a of only one alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , it is simple to show that the objective function of mathematical program  (1) , the EV goal, achieves its maximum value by assigning all probability mass to the single alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , and none to the remaining alternatives in  
                               C 
                            . Essentially, any probability mass assigned to an alternative not in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             reduces the objective function; hence, all probability mass must be allocated to  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             in an optimal solution. Since there is only one alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , assign all probability mass to it. This solution satisfies the constraints of  (1) , so the optimal probability solution is given by   (5d) .

The more general case  (3) b, in which there are two or more alternatives in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , leads to a much more complex solution for the optimal choice probabilities. Assigning any probability mass to alternatives not in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             will reduce the objective function compared to shifting that mass to an alternative in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            , hence it is suboptimal to assign  any  probability mass outside  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                            . On the other hand,  any  probability distribution among the alternatives in  
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                               
                             yields the same maximum objective function value. Hence, expression  (5e)  describes a class of feasible probability distributions that maximize   (1) . □

Theorem 1  establishes that optimization problem  (3) , as an expression of a decision making process, encompasses three decision making modes: (a) two single goal cases (cases 1 and 3 emphasizing exclusively one or the other of the choice set entropy and expected value goals, respectively) and (b) a two-objective case, where the choice set entropy and expected value goals are mixed according to the combination weight  
                         
                            
                               w 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                      . In the former cases, limiting choice probability distributions are obtained; in the latter case, the MNL (multinomial logit) model results. Of course, the two single goal cases are extreme noninferior solutions to a vector optimization of the two goals, but with the idea of exploitation versus exploration in mind, we might expect the mixed goal case to prevail. In fact, we have the following result (adapted from  Chankong and Haimes (1983, Theorem 4.11) ): If  
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               C 
                            
                         
                       solves the weighting problem with  
                         0 
                         < 
                         
                            
                               w 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                         < 
                         1 
                      , then  
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               C 
                            
                         
                       is a noninferior solution of the vector optimization problem; thus, mixed exploitation–exploration always gives a noninferior solution to the original problem. 
                         2 
                      
                      
                         2 
                         Note also that  Chankong and Haimes (1983, Theorem 4.11)  show that the converse of this result holds except for certain extreme solutions which they call  improper .

                       Two points can be made about these expressions. Firstly, as noted before, in  (3)  all quantities involved in the solution for the optimal value of the Lagrange multiplier  (7a)  are defined by the DM, not the researcher, distinguishing this solution method from those based on, say, a threshold constraint on the entropy goal. Secondly, in a somewhat restricted form in which

                         C 
                      , quantity  (7b)  is widely known as the “logsum” or “inclusive value” in literatures employing the random utility derivation of the MNL model (see, e.g.,  Ben-Akiva and Lerman (1985) ). In that framework, it arises as the expectation of the maximum of independently and identically distributed Type I extreme value distributions (more on this below). In our derivation, it does not arise as the result of the stochastic specification of some set of random variates, rather it is an intermediate quantity arising from the solution to the deterministic scalar optimization representation of the decision process.

Theorem 2  establishes that the logsum (or inclusive value) is equal to the maximum value of the rescaled objective function  
                         
                            
                               Λ 
                            
                            
                               ̄ 
                            
                         
                         
                            ( 
                            p 
                            , 
                            C 
                            ) 
                         
                       of  (4b) . This result is therefore of special importance because it establishes a direct link between the random utility derivation of the MNL model (expression  (8) ) and its derivation from  (3) . Under the condition that the scale factor  
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                       is equal to the relative importance of the expected value goal in decision problem  (3) , the two approaches are fully equivalent.

Expression  (10)  contains an important identity embodied in the MNL model that holds regardless of the conceptual basis for the model’s derivation: with  
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               ∗ 
                            
                         
                       the choice probability vector at the maximum, the logsum (inclusive value) is equal to the sum of the expected (weighted) utility and a constant times the entropy of the set, where the constant equals the inverse of the importance of the choice set entropy goal relative to that of the expected value goal. This expression is very similar in structure to the utility of gambling representation previously mentioned (e.g.,  Luce et al. (2009) ), and testifies to the MNL’s underlying interpretation as the outcome of a specific dual-goal process.

To us, one of the most significant consequences of deriving the multinomial logit (MNL) model  (5b)  from the scalar optimization problem  (3)  is that no foundational stochastic assumptions were necessary; such stochastic assumptions are required when deriving the model from the random utility (RU) perspective. A corollary to this is that in the proposed approach the total value  
                         
                            
                               V 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       does not need to be decomposed in some fashion into a systematic component and a stochastic one. As a consequence, from a process perspective, formulation  (3)  can be understood to represent a DM whose choices are inherently probabilistic, rather than viewed as such due to analyst ignorance, which is the usual Economist’s interpretation of the RU approach.

Also, depending upon the approach used to derive the MNL model, the scale factor can be interpreted differently. In the random utility derivation the scale factor  
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                       (see expressions  (10) a–b above) reflects the contribution of the stochastic utility relative to the total utility, and is known to be inversely proportional to the variance of stochastic utility (specifically, for the Gumbel distribution the variance is  
                         
                            
                               σ 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         
                            
                               π 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         / 
                         6 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      ; see, e.g.,  Ben-Akiva and Lerman (1985) ). In contrast, in the derivation of the choice probabilities stated in  Theorem 1 , the scalar  
                         
                            
                               r 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                       expresses the relative contribution of an alternative’s value to the EV component of the objective function of  (3)  compared to its CSE component, and thus captures the relative desire for exploitation versus exploration.

Note that in both the RU and our multi-objective approaches, “heteroscedastic” versions of the MNL can be derived: in the former, the assumption of identical distributions is relaxed to allow for alternative-specific scale factors  
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               C 
                            
                         
                       (see  Swait (2006) , for derivation details). In the proposed approach  (3) , we would have to specify that the relative importance of the EV and CSE goals become alternative-specific. Details of this model derivation are left to the reader.

Identification conditions for  (5b)  are, of course, the same as for the MNL derived from random utility theory. Since probabilities are established from differences in values, an arbitrary origin must be established—e.g. one option-specific constant in the value functions must be set to a constant (say, zero), and exogenous variables that contribute to intercept shifts must also have a base alternative. In a single set  
                         C 
                      , there is no possibility of identifying the  
                         
                            
                               τ 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               C 
                            
                         
                       thresholds, so they must be set to a common constant value (say, zero). However, the threshold is maintained in the model for purposes of developing choice models in which there are multiple  
                         C 
                      ’s. The values  
                         
                            
                               V 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       are only identified up to a scalar, specifically ( 
                         
                            
                               r 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                         
                            
                               κ 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               C 
                            
                         
                      ). It will therefore be necessary to impose normalization conditions during statistical estimation of these models.

The introduction of the  
                         
                            
                               τ 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               C 
                            
                         
                       “soft” constraint allows a connection to be drawn between the MNL model given in  (5b)  and the general “soft” constraint random utility approach discussed in  Swait (2001a) . Both are based on incorporating constraint violations into the objective function, allowing for the possibility of violations at a cost to the overall objective. The major difference, however, is that model  (5b)  imposes a requirement on the values (i.e. utilities) of alternatives in the choice set, whereas the approach in  Swait (2001a)  is formulated on the basis of attribute requirements (e.g. price below some maximum) and restrictions imposed on general attribute-based functions (e.g. daily expenditure on a car rental below some total). At the cost of greater complexity, decision problem  (3)  can be extended to include such attribute-based requirements.

In the prior section we motivated the optimization of a scalar objective function composed of the convex combination of two particular goals as a means of generating noninferior solutions to a vector optimization decision problem; the solution to that decision problem proves to be choice probabilities of the MNL form. In this section we demonstrate the power and flexibility of the multi-goal approach by using it to derive more general probabilistic choice models. We do so with two examples:

Prior choice experience : this is a straightforward extension of the basic model that allows for the inclusion of prior experience, by introducing a third goal-type, targeting;

Representation of multi-stage decision processes : using the proposed method to derive a popular variant of the MNL, the nested logit, we are able to demonstrate that it is a very general procedure that can describe decision processes that have multiple stages.

Prior choice experience

Choice probability model  (13)  is a straightforward, though behaviorally significant, generalization of the MNL model  (5b) . It has counterparts in the literature, particularly with respect to its dependence on a base condition. The Incremental MNL model ( Ben-Akiva & Lerman, 1985 , p. 114) has the exact form of  (13) , though the exponentiated arguments are interpreted as  changes  in utility with respect to a base situation for which choice probabilities  
                         q 
                       are known (they represent an initial state-of-the-world around which changes are studied). Independently,  Cheremukhin et al. (2013)  derive this model as a special case of the static version of a dynamic choice process with information processing costs (see their  Theorem 5 ) with the prior probabilities being interpreted as initial conditions or biases influencing the DM in a dynamic choice setting. Additionally, one can motivate  (13)  from other perspectives: e.g., the  
                         q 
                      ’s can represent social influences from the DM’s reference group in the form of observed or perceived group behaviors.

The nested logit model—multi-stage decision processes

The second extension to the basic multi-goal approach we examine deals with the nested logit model. In the random utility perspective, the possibility that the standard MNL model’s built-in assumption of independence of irrelevant alternatives (IIA) may not hold empirically is widely recognized: the relative odds of choosing alternative  
                         i 
                       over  
                         j 
                       may not be independent of a third alternative  
                         k 
                      , for  
                         i 
                      ,  
                         j 
                      ,  
                         k 
                         ∈ 
                         C 
                      . The nested logit model is motivated by this need to relax the IIA assumption, and does so within the random utility framework by postulating that unobserved stochastic effects are shared by sub-groups/clusters/nests of alternatives. These nests are specified as follows: choice set  
                         C 
                       is partitioned  a priori  into  
                         G 
                         ≥ 
                         2 
                       mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive subsets  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               g 
                            
                         
                      . Each alternative  
                         i 
                         ∈ 
                         C 
                       therefore belongs to one and only one cluster, denoted  
                         g 
                         
                            ( 
                            i 
                            ) 
                         
                      ; consequently, for  
                         i 
                         , 
                         j 
                         ∈ 
                         C 
                         , 
                         g 
                         
                            ( 
                            i 
                            ) 
                         
                         = 
                         g 
                         
                            ( 
                            j 
                            ) 
                         
                       iff  
                         i 
                       and  
                         j 
                       belong to a common cluster.  Fig. 1 
                       presents this scheme diagrammatically.

Now consider the following optimization problem, first stated, then the notation described:  
                         
                            (14) 
                            
                               
                                  
                                     [

Theorem 5  is important for a number of reasons. From a behavioral and theoretical perspective it is significant that, under any conditions at all, two very different solution strategies to the underlying decision problem (one globally maximizing and the other locally maximizing) yield the same choice probabilities. Given the core role that the MNL model plays in the generalized extreme value (GEV) family of choice models ( McFadden, 1976 ), one is led to speculate that this equivalence of local and global optimizing behavior is deeply embedded in the GEV family of models. This is an interesting direction for future research, which we discuss further in the final section of the paper.

It is just as significant that the condition for the equality of the local and global solutions of the nested logit hinges crucially on the inclusive value quantity  (17) . Early work in the transport literature ( Ben-Akiva, 1973; Williams, 1977 ) identified the inclusive value as the necessary link variable between MNL sub-models in forecasting systems so as to yield internally consistent predictions. For example, the use of simple or weighted averages of utilities as link variables between MNL components was shown to produce counterintuitive predictions under certain conditions. The approaches used in these references to demonstrate the role of the inclusive value in linking MNL models were based on empirical observation and/or properties of groups of extreme value density functions.  Theorem 5  goes beyond these motivations by establishing that the inclusive value is the  only  link that correctly ties together MNL sub-models in a theoretically consistent fashion that has a behavioral goal-driven interpretation. The global vs. local perspectives that are suggested by the multi-objective framework enable a relatively straightforward verification of equivalence conditions between the perspectives, something which was not so easily done on the basis of the prior work cited above.

A final reason for the significance of this theorem lies in the common empirical interpretation given to the nested logit model. As noted before, the original impetus behind the development of this model was the desire to relax the IIA assumption. Within the random utility framework, the notion of IIA violations is made possible by introducing the prospect that the options within a cluster (or nests) share unobserved components of utility. However, the empirical interpretation of nested logit models almost always devolves into a discussion of decision sequences. To caricaturize this generally, statements of the type “decision makers first select between the private and public categories of transport, then decide within each group of modes which one to use for work trips” are used to describe modeling results. Interestingly, such characterizations of decision processes are quite consistent with the “divide-and-conquer” or local solution strategy to the scalar optimization problem, but are not at all consistent with the jointly dependent stochastic specification of the nested logit model in the random utility perspective, except when the local and global solutions agree ( Theorem 5 ). The multi-goal approach we propose, and  Theorem 5  in particular, establishes that the decision process interpretation is not only intuitive, but also a desirable one for the nested logit specification.

This paper has developed a relatively general framework to characterize goal-driven behaviors by formulating a scalar optimization equivalent to a multi-goal vector optimization. In this way, it is able to take inherently deterministic valuations and associate them with probabilistic choice. We have exemplified three classes of high-level goals which might be variously characterized as (1) exploitation (expectations of valuations), (2) exploration (via measures of entropy) and (3) targeting (adherence to exogenous probability distributions). Combinations of two or more such goals are straightforwardly incorporated into the scalar optimization framework.

The proposed framework, when implemented as here with the combination of exploitation and exploration goals, has been shown to lead to the well-known multinomial logit (MNL) model of choice (see expression  (5b) ). We can thus interpret this workhorse discrete choice model as characteristic of the outcome of goal-driven behavior that originates from balanced pursuit of both goals (i.e., exploitation and exploration). The balance is parametrically expressed via the convex combination of the two goal functions using weight  
                         
                            
                               w 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                         ∈ 
                         
                            [ 
                            0 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ] 
                         
                      . Contrary to other approaches of deriving the MNL model (e.g. random utility), this formal dual-goal optimization approach enables us to interpret the scale factor of the MNL as the result of a behavioral sub-process (goal balancing); this specificity of interpretation should promote future experimental testing of the general approach to explore its axiomatic premises.

Going beyond the basic MNL model, we have also demonstrated that a somewhat more complex version of the weighted goal maximization framework yields the nested logit model, provided “local” and “global” weighted maximization give the same choice probabilities over the available options. This model results from the sequential partitioning of the full choice set  
                         C 
                       into  
                         G 
                       mutually exclusive and exhaustive subsets  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               g 
                            
                         
                      ; choice of an option arises from choosing first a subset  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               g 
                            
                         
                      , then choosing an option from that subset. This partitioning strategy is a very general “divide-and-conquer” problem-solving technique, and thus points to the potentially much wider applicability of the multi-goal framework we have presented.

This association between the proposed perspective of multi-goal optimization and the nested logit model is also suggestive that the framework may be viewed as the essential generating theme for various more complex models in the generalized extreme value (GEV) class of random utility models, of which the MNL and nested logit models are members. The GEV class of choice models (see  McFadden (1978) , for the basic characterization of the class) can be succinctly summarized through the following choice probability statement (see  Ben-Akiva and Lerman (1985, pp. 126–128) ):

The ease with which it is possible to obtain choice probabilities  (25)  from our decompositional approach belies the power and generality of the result. The choice modeling literatures in transportation and related areas give a number of other exemplars of the GEV class of choice models, each of which can be obtained from  (25)  and a specific generation function: among others, we mention the generalized nested logit ( Wen & Koppelman, 2001 ), the choice set generation logit (GenL,  Swait (2001b) ), and the ordered GEV ( Small (1987) ; see below).

There is a more substantive implication of the derivation of  (25)  from our decompositional approach, and of the interpretation of the nested logit model as arising, equivalently, from global optimization or a “divide-and-conquer” approach to smaller subproblems. Namely, that it is likely that numerous significantly more complex GEV models can be viewed from this decompositional perspective. For example, the ordered GEV model ( Small, 1987 ) appears to be similarly decomposable, with a more complex decomposition of the choice probabilities and a larger set of inclusive value restrictions; in that model alternatives are correlated if they are “close together” on some natural ordering. This example, along with our result on the nested logit, suggests the following open problem: can any model in the GEV class that can be written in a recursive (nested) form in terms of sets of alternatives and choice probabilities be characterized from our decompositional perspective? Such models do not cover the whole GEV class, but certainly form an important subclass.

One of the important conceptual generalizations of the basic MNL probabilistic choice model during the past decade has been its Mixed Logit extension ( McFadden & Train, 2000 ).                       , the DM can display heterogeneous preferences that follow the distribution. Multiple trials for the DM are necessary to identify weights  
                         
                            
                               β 
                            
                            
                               ̄ 
                            
                         
                       and the deep parameters of the distribution  
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            
                               ζ 
                            
                         
                      .

In the previous paragraphs we touched upon the idea of preference evolution by considering the possibility that preferences can vary over trials (note also our earlier discussion of reinforcement learning in the introductory section of the paper). Specifically, over any sequentially indexed process (e.g., trials, time, path in space)

                      , might change, even from stochastic to (more) deterministic, according to any number of evolutionary processes or constraining factors (e.g., learning, reinforcement, satiation, stochastic shocks, risk preference, expectations, decision effort limits). We are currently undertaking the formalization of certain of these processes within the framework presented in this article, along the lines of  Swait, Adamowicz, and van Buren (2004) .

The exploitation (or expected value) goal, as we have implemented it, describes the general motivation of obtaining a good/great/best outcome from the choice process. In our formulation, we have allowed the value to be a general measure of attractiveness, net of costs or threshold requirements. Other goals of a similar mathematical structure are certainly feasible, as in the desire to minimize regret (say, with respect to an outside alternative with known value  
                         V 
                      ). For example, define “regret” generated from choosing an alternative as the degree to which its value is smaller than an outside alternative (whether real or ideal). Thus, the regret goal might be defined as follows:

                       Essentially, choice is random among the alternatives whose values exceed the threshold. Intuitively, direct consideration of the ES optimization problem, expression  (31) , leads to this same solution, but the mathematical proof of that is less straightforward. Thus, the mixture of goals actually allows for a simpler solution method to obtain the choice probabilities.

We have represented the exploration goal by entropy (Shannon information), which parallels the assumption in essentially all of the related results in the literature. As in that literature, our ability to derive closed form results for choice probabilities depends crucially on such an entropy term mixed with a (expected) utility term that is linear in the choice probabilities. This might suggest that one cannot go beyond this “vanilla” assumption and develop more detailed models of the variety seeking component; we mention two possibilities for doing so.

Cheremukhin et al. (2013)  focus on the formulation of a dynamic model of choice, the static version of which corresponds to the maximization of the difference between an expected value term and a cost term. The latter is a convex function of the mutual information between the response probabilities and a prior distribution of such response probabilities. These authors use Kullback–Leibler’s information measure for the mutual information function, just as we have in some of our developments for the Adherence or Targeting goal (see expression  (11) ). By using various different convex cost functions, they are able to approximate numerous classic probabilistic choice models, with the focus on binary choice; these models include the probit, logit, and tremble models. They do not explore results parallelling ours on the GEV class.

                       This is a generalization of the representation we use in the body of the paper, and when

However, in general, for it to be identifiably different to the form assumed earlier in our article requires the separate identification of the properties of the outcomes  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       and the events  
                         
                            
                               E 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                      . The beginning on such a framework might be to consider the  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       to be quantitative aspects of the options and the  
                         
                            
                               E 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       to be qualitative aspects of the options. For example, if the options were different yoghurts, one conceptualization of the  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       would be as a set of ordered  quantitative attributes  (such as price, calories, fat content) and of the  
                         
                            
                               E 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       as a set of unordered  qualitative features  (such as flavour, packaging, brand); for a restaurant, the two sets of properties could be price, size, and caloric content of meals versus flavour, ambience, and service. A rather different conceptualization of the second set of properties is as a set of events directly associated with the choice made, separate from the properties of the chosen option—for instance, the event of a concert being canceled may have a (emotional) value,  per se , independent of any of the (quantifiable) consequences of the cancelation, such as the cost of the non-refundable ticket. Whatever the conceptualization, the inset entropy formulation framework depends crucially on the idea that there are two distinct sets of properties. The first set of properties satisfies specified ordinal (quantifiable) properties and only appear in the utility component; the second set of properties only appear in the variety seeking (inset entropy) component and are assumed to be representable as  sets —as in the usual idea of  (qualitative) features , as employed in Tversky’s  elimination by aspects (EBA)  model ( Tversky, 1972a,b ).

Finally, in addition to satisfactorily motivating representation  (37)  in our variety seeking context, it would be of interest to axiomatize it in the utility of gambling interpretation (where we would have  
                         
                            
                               w 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                         ≠ 
                         0 
                       and independent of  
                         C 
                      )—the latter is posed as an open problem by  Ng et al. (2007) .
